0817439390|1.0|The author of this book has a very particular style that I noticed in some other do-it-yourself American books. This book is written in a very informal language and is filled with anecdotes, metaphors, and other techniques that are supposed to help you understand exposure.    However, presenting pre-digested information in this way assumes that the reader does not have big aspirations and their understanding capabilities are low. If you like this spoon-feeding style, then you may like this book. I did not. I found it very annoying that whenever I was hoping to get some genuine and technical information, I got a pre-digested inaccurate metaphor. For instance, the author explains the ISO feature by comparing it to worker bees: ISO 100 = 100 working bees that carry light, ISO 200 = 200 bees and more light. To me this information explains nothing, it only provides a silly rule of thumb; that is not enough to understand photography. I believe that to reach a good understanding of photography you need to go beyond such simplistic heuristics and get to know a bit more of the technical side and receive in-depth explanations.    If you really want to gain an understanding of your digital camera and correct exposure, this book is not the best choice.    A wonderful series of books written in a compelling and professional, yet accessible to a beginner, way is by Harold Davis.

B00004TR2G|5.0|for the real original soundtrack recording of JAWS to come out. It took twenty-five years but it finally happened  and I could not have been happier. I bought the original MCA album (actually, I was only 13 in 1975 so my mom got it for me) and was both happy and disappointed. Happy that I actually had the music from the film and not so happy that it wasn't the \\"whole damn thing.\\" It was only Williams' re-recording of portions he selected and upon hearing them I thought it was a bit different than what I heard in the film. only later I found out why.    JAWS did a couple of things for me. First, it made me aware of film music and the power it can hold and I considered Williams my favorite composer until a year later. Second, it made me aware that Robert Shaw is still my all-time favorite actor. But here the music must take front and center.    It is truly one of the most iconic musical compositions of the last century that became so heavily entrenched in popular culture that virtually everyone knows this music when they hear it. John Williams really hit it out of the park and the Decca release of the actual session performances is one of the most significant album releases of 2000.  After scoring perhaps the three most successful disaster films of the 1970s it was only with his music for JAWS did  Williams become a force to be reckoned with. The original recordings contain a more raw primal element in the performances with the most ominous being the two note shark ostinato which is the main theme of the film. It was so  simple in execution and so effective that it earned an Academy Award despite its similarity to Bernard Herrmann's jarring string motifs from PSYCHO.    JAWS is a much more accessible film score in my opinion and has reached a far-reaching audience and rightfully so.  While some may argue that the score is not really an easy listen in its complete form then try listening to Elliot  Goldenthal's ALIEN 3 music. The music that Williams composed here is much more melodically enjoyable and it is easy to hear some homages to Korngold in some of the shark hunt music (\\"Man Against Beast\\" and \\"The Great Shark Chase\\"). I always termed Williams as more of a traditionalist in regards to his scoring style as opposed to the more modernist leanings of my top favorites Jerry Goldsmith and Alex North. By traditionalist I mean that a lot of his scores are leitmotif driven as the composers of Hollywood's Golden Age were.    Not that its a bad thing. I personally think that Williams best works are this score, BLACK SUNDAY, THE FURY, and  SUPERMAN. His music for JAWS displays his more groundbreaking ideas: the simplicity and primal power of the shark theme, the eerieness of \\"Quint's Tale\\", and the swashbuckling shark hunt music. His music here has a rawness that his later scores after STAR WARS no longer seem to have. Like any true artist his style has changed over the years and I find that I prefer his fresher sound in scores such as this one and BLACK SUNDAY. Personally, I don't find the complete score a hard listen at all. It even features some music that wasn't even used in the finished cut of the film so it comes as a pleasant surprise. Nowadays I find a lot of music in his scores to be a bit on the decorative side but here he really got at the guts of the film which is what every composer working in film is supposed to do. JAWS may well be one of the few perfect scores written for a film and works ingeniously well while at the same time stands on its own as a serious musical work.    For those who think his music for JAWS 2 was better I strongly disagree. The music was a bit over the top given some of the plot elements of the sequel but it just doesn't hold the raw power of the original. The Decca release should be in any self-respecting film music lovers collection. They just don't write 'em like this anymore!!
B004UXUXGE|4.0|&lt;strong>Cave of Forgotten Dreams&lt;/strong> (Werner Herzog, 2010)<br /><br />Every review of &lt;em>Cave of Forgotten Dreams&lt;/em>, I think, has mentioned at some point that (a) THIS is what 3D is really meant for, not that stuff-popping-out-at-you gig, and (b) Herzog's use of it here is nothing short of revolutionary. I will nod at it and movie on, save one big nag I'll get to presently, but I'm kind of stuck without it. I saw the movie a week ago, and the more I've thought about it since, the more I've come to the conclusion that the reason so many column inches have been focused on Herzog's just-shy-of-orgasmic use of 3D is because, when it gets right down to it, there's not a great deal else to say about this movie. Don't get me wrong, that doesn't mean you shouldn't go see it; you should, and you should do so at the earliest opportunity you have, and believe me, this is one you absolutely &lt;em>must&lt;/em> see in 3D. It will probably be about a dozen times better on the big screen, too. But it is not without flaws.<br /><br />This is a documentary in the purest sense of the word, at its best moments--it is a document of the discovery and exploration of Chauvet Cavern, home to the earliest known cave paintings on the planet. This is partially, Herzog explains, because he and his crew had very little time in the cavern, were made to stay on two-foot-wide metal walkways during shooting, and had to use a primitive, if you'll pardon the pun, light system. As a result, there is a great deal here that looks hasty, and there is very little artistry to it. This is, of course, belied by the 3D, but it's a convincing illusion if you look past that particular hole, and I love the air of authenticity it lends. When Herzog keeps his focus on the cave itself, this is Herzog's best work, and I say that as someone who has four of Herzog's films on my list of the best ever made. Yes, it's that good.<br /><br />On the other hand, as any veteran Herzog fan knows, \\"Herzog\\" and \\"focus\\" are often ill-used when spoken in the same breath, and that particular malady is just as present here as it usually is. About half the movie focuses on the cave itself. The rest focuses on Herzog's usual obsession, a band of oddballs who have formed around it, and those parts are much less about the cave than they are about the oddballs. These parts are distracting, especially in the 3D environment; what works so well in depicting cave walls is less useful when we're watching someone being interviewed.<br /><br />And now that nag I mentioned: there are certain times when the 3D isn't quite on. Okay, I correct myself; there are times when it just flies way outside the realms of acceptable. The first time you'll notice this is in the film's first few minutes, as the crew are walking up the path to the cave entrance. Focus (or, if you can help it, avoid focusing) on the wooden slats through which the rope guiderails run. As they turn a corner, one in the bottom left of the screen gets fuzzy enough to (in me, at least) induce an instant headache. That's the first time it happens, but not the last; offhand, I can think of at least three more points during the movie when the 3D also loses focus.<br /><br />Still, the good of this movie far, far outweighs the bad. The cave paintings themselves are stunning, and the 3D instills a sense of wonder that is impossible to get across in words; if you've ever heard someone describe visiting Ayers Rock for the first time, and getting a true sense of the massiveness of it that you just can't get from pictures, well, it's kinda like that, but different. If Herzog had simply given me a wordless ninety minutes of that, I probably would have immediately sent this film into my top 100. Its weaknesses prevent that, but it is still, when it brings its A game, an outstanding piece of work. See it as soon as you can. *** 1/2in the same breath, and that particular malady is just as present here as it usually is. About half the movie focuses on the cave itself. B004LWZW24|4.0|Insidious (James Wan, 2011)<br /><br />The last time director James Wan and screenwriter Leigh Whannell got together, we got the criminally-underrated 2007 fright flick Dead Silence. It's a fantastic little movie, scary in all the right places, and pretty much no one saw it. The reason for that is the film they collaborated on before that--Saw. Well, okay, not Saw itself, but the series of increasingly-unwatchable sequels that came after it. Wan had nothing to do with them save throwing money at whoever was working on them at the time (Whannell wrote the next couple before dropping out, at which point he adopted Wan's \\"we'll make money off it\\" stance and became another executive producer). Wan and Whannell's names will forever be linked with the Saw franchise, despite neither one of them having had a firsthand effect on a Saw movie since Saw III, when Melton and Dunstan (The Collector) took over Whannell's writing duties, and the series abandoned any pretense of being other than the gory soap opera it is. Given all that, it's sometimes hard to remember that the traps and the gore in the original film were nothing more than accents concealing a perfectly-scripted, perfectly-paced mystery. For all that Saw took the roughie genre and dragged it into the mainstream, essentially inventing \\"torture porn\\", go back and rewatch the first movie--it isn't torture porn at all. Then came Dead Silence, which about half the time I'm convinced no one but me has ever seen. (Rectify that at your earliest convenience.) And so we come to Insidious, the third collaboration between the two. It is much more Dead Silence than it is Saw, and because of that, I'm quite surprised at the enthusiastic reception it got on the festival circuit. The reaction from the critics has been more predictable; many hated it. (As I write this, its Rotten Tomatoes rating is 60%; it's teetering on the \\"fresh\\" threshold.) I think it's possible a number of them missed something, and it's something very important to one's enjoyment of the film, in my regard: James Wan has jumped on the retro-horror bandwagon that came into being as (I have often suspected) a reaction to Hollywood's current, relentless remaking of old horror films under Michael Bay and Roy Lee's aegides. The thinking seems to be, \\"if people are going to go see old horror films, why don't we make new horror films that seem like old horror films?\\". The trend brought us two of the best films of 2009 in any genre, not just horror: Sam Raimi's Drag Me to Hell and Ti West's The House of the Devil. Insidious runs straight through those same veins, and of all the critics I've read on this issue, only AP's Christy Lemire has gotten it half-right, and she's the best anyone's done so far. More on that in a few.<br /><br />Plot: the Lambert family have just moved into a new house. We have dad, Josh (Hard Candy's Patrick Wilson), a schoolteacher; mom, Renai (28 Weeks Later...'s Rose Byrne), who's taking time off from corporate life (we never do find out what she does there) in order to focus on a nascent musical career we are to infer she gave up to get married and have kids; two sons, Foster (The Hangover's Andrew Astor) and Dalton (Little Children's Ty Simpkins, and I'm amazed this kid hasn't found superstar status yet), and the new baby. The first night, while everyone's just kind of hanging around, Dalton wanders up into the attic, which we already know is creepy because Renai was up there previous. He slips and falls, banging his head. Seems okay, but the next morning, he doesn't wake up. Hospital stay, etc., no one can figure out what's wrong with him. Until, that is, Josh's mom Lorraine (the always-welcome Barbara Hershey, recently of Black Swan) hooks Josh up with Elise Rainier (Lin Shaye, known primarily as a comedic actress, but her horror pedigree is a mile long and stretches right back to the original A Nightmare on Elm Street). It is Elise who delivers the movie's tagline, which I think was supposed to be aspoiler, but ended up in every trailer ever released for the film: \\"it's not the house that's haunted. It's your son.\\"<br /><br />Lemire mentioned in her review (and in dialogue on Ebert Presents At the Movies with mubi.com's Ignatiy Vishnevetsky, who loathed it) that it's \\"basically a remake of Poltergeist\\". As I said before, she's half-right. It's very much informed by Poltergeist, with Shaye taking Zelda Rubinstein's role, but that wasn't Whannell's only source material here. The movie is equally informed by Sidney J. Furie's 1980 frightfest The Entity, and if you want to get where Wan and Whannell, especially the latter, are at with this movie, that's the critical component. The Entity was one of the best, and arguably one of the scariest, horror films of the eighties, and it basically got ignored because it was doing supernatural horror during the golden age of the slasher film. (Also, telling: who starred in The Entity? If you said \\"Barbara Hershey\\", you get the gold star. Including her in &lt;em>Insidious&lt;/em> was, I strongly suspect, not a random casting decision.) The Entity has not been nearly as influential as it should have been because of that, and so it's not as easily detected as Poltergeist as an influence. But watch The Entity and then watch Insidious and tell me you can't see where some of those shots came from. The best retro horror is retro horror that captures the feel of the seventies and early eighties while being rooted in the present day; The House of the Devil was a pure-D throwback to seventies satanic panic pictures with the cynical cultural awareness/self-obliviousness of the modern teenager subbing in the lead role. Insidious went the same way, except it's the modern family instead of the modern teenager.<br /><br />One of the other things we forgot as the Saw franchise degenerated was how subtle a screenwriter Leigh Whannell can be. That's something that didn't really resurface in Dead Silence, and thus I'm ashamed to say I forgot it as well. But check out the way, for example, Patrick Wilson's character is set up. Wilson is at his best when, as in Hard Candy, he's playing a sleazeball. And that's exactly the setup we get. We're expecting a sleazeball. You know exactly where Whannell is going with the elisions and the outright lies. What makes it all the more intelligent is that Renai knows it, too, but is also smart enough, and has been married to Josh long enough, to see through it. That's multi-layered storytelling for you right there, and it's well-done indeed. Now here's the thing: that sort of subtlety is, for obvious reasons, more easily detected in the first half of the film, which is basically all setup. (Even Vishnevetsky said, however reluctantly, that the first half of the movie was better.) But it doesn't just go away when we get to all the supernatural-action stuff. Everyone and their mother has been blasting the ending of this movie, and now that I'm thinking about it, I'm wondering if the problem wasn't Whannell's subtlety, but audience expectations that that subtlety had gone away. Which makes no sense; think about the Big Reveal scene in Saw, where it all came together and we finally got Jigsaw's identity (it's still one of my favorite Big Reveal scenes ever), and it was blessedly obvious (thanks to the overbearing montage that immediately followed it) how subtle all that setup had been. Wan and Whannell got rid of the montage here, thankfully, though they couldn't resist one flashback scene that wasn't necessary. Are you telling me you didn't see it all in retrospect, or that you didn't think it worked? Because it was absolutely there. Whether it worked or not is personal preference, of course. I thought it worked very well. (I also wonder how much influence producer Oren Peli had over the ending, which is a little too close to the ending of Paranormal Activity 2 to be a coincidence.) More to the point, would you have preferred the other, more obvious, ending? 'cause to methat would have felt like a major cop-out. (Oh, one other retro touch--the frenzied eating of spaghetti. Where have we seen that before?)<br /><br />In short--I liked this movie. I think I probably liked it a lot better than you did, but I also think that if you watch it another time or two, knowing the ending, you may pick up on a lot more of the reasons I liked it as much as I did. Hey, it's worth a shot. As far as the movies I compared it to above, it's not in the same league as The Entity, but it's on the same level, quality-wise, as Dead Silence and Poltergeist. If you haven't seen it yet, be aware this isn't your basic fright flick; you'll want to pay slightly closer attention than you otherwise might. *** 1/2example, Patrick Wilson's character is set up. 0385333846|4.0|Billy Pilgrim is the definition of a spectacle. With his azure shower curtain, fur muff, and silver boots, he is an embarrassment to the US Army as he marches into the Dresden Prisoner of War Camp. In Kurt Vonnegut's cross genre classic, Slaughterhouse Five, Vonnegut approaches the anti-war novel in a new light. Vonnegut's story focuses on the life of Billy Pilgrim, a slightly insane optometrist who believes he was abducted by aliens and now has the ability to time travel. Throughout the novel, Billy travels through time, mainly focusing on his time as a POW in Germany and Dresden during WW II.     In its simplest form, Slaughterhouse Five is an Anti-War book. Vonnegut achieves this, by highlighting the pointlessness of war and the easiness of death. Vonnegut however, fails to acknowledge how one must deal with war. Yes, there is pain and suffering, but how does one come back from that? Billy Pilgrim, embodies this idea within himself, as he has a seemingly successful life after the war, but he unable to enjoy It as he becomes obsessed with the idea that he was abducted by Tralfamadorians. Is Vonnegut saying that to move past the horrors of war, you need to envelope your mind in something else to point of insanity? Even the phrase \\"So it goes\\" implies a passive acceptance that things are the way they are. Vonnegut succeeds in the age old argument that war is evil, and pushes the envelope by highlighting the psychological effects after war. Vonnegut, however, fails to provide any inspiration or guide as to prevent wars in the future.     Outside of its anti-war message, Slaughterhouse Five is a strange but enjoyable read, as Vonnegut dark satire provides a new perspective and outlook for the reader.

1932100660|1.0|At the start of the book there's talk about an experiment with \\"protein\\" and mice. And Campbell gives a superficially persuasive account of how this \\"protein\\" was profoundly to blame for switching cancer on and off in the experiments.     He seemed to be explaining things in a straight-forward and honest way. Leaving nothing out, careful to reference to his own precision and objectivity as a scientist not to get carried away by the findings. I was blown away by the implications and suggestions of these things he was describing. Little did I realize until later that this was all a pack of lies.     I also thought that this was a perfect \\"test\\" to see whether he was credible, whether he was reliable or not, by looking up the internet later. He didn't mention animal protein at this time. In fact I was really upset and desperately searched the index to see if my beloved fish and peanuts were okay in this. I thought that if this food-terrorism could be substantiated then I would be fully on board. If however he was merely a charlatan then he would be leaving out things in this study.     Turns out I didn't even have to wait to get on the internet to see if he was full of it or not. A couple of pages later and he casually mentions that all of these experiments were done using CASEIN. Not JUST ANY PROTEIN. Not CONTROLLING for different times of protein. Not whey protein here, casein there, plant protein over there. It's ridiculous. I never drink cow's milk and very, very rarely get casein. It's his inability to tell an important point from a totally unimportant one that got me most of all there. I was well aware that casein was a bad protein to be avoided, though perhaps not with the cancer shock that he's trying to induce considering that people who drink milk have in some studies a LOWER rate of cancer. And this was only after dosage with large amounts of aflatoxin.     I later found out that even the casein helped protect against the aflatoxin. But Campbell hid that. And you have to ask WHY. I agree with a lot of what Campbell stands for in diet, but why does he feel such a need to hide and distort things?       There is a blog which completely dissects the data of the China Study here: [...]    I have read Campbell's responses to these and other commentaries and they basically amount to: 1) You don't understand statistics. 2) This was just my \\"story\\" of things that seemed to come together for me personally.     The first one is ad hominem, and then second basically admits that this whole thing is just a fictional or anecdotal account of things, his informal \\"story\\" of how things were. But I still don't understand how this allows him to ignore data that doesn't fit with his theory, to just leave them out of the book altogether.      He says that he considered his options when writing it: 1) To give a one-sided view of the data 2) To give an all-sides account without bias 3) To give his \\"personal story\\" of how it came about that he would come to believe in this idea and this diet.     In MY opinion, either of the first two would be good. I certainly don't mind someone advocating a particular diet SO LONG AS THEY ALSO GIVE THE ARGUMENTS THAT GO AGAINST THEM!!!!! Not try and hide them.     But he said that he would take the third option, and that when the data didn't fit properly... he would just put it down to experimental error because he knew the way the general numbers were going anyway, and he wouldn't mention it.         I wonder if it could rather be that Campbell's study was a failure and he just couldn't accept that. This could have been a great book for bringing diet into the 21st century... it could show how unreliable cross-studies of populations are. But instead Campbell chose to try to exhert his own opinions on things.     My own diet is very high on fruit/vegetables and low on animal protein, so I'm not one of those staunch advocates of very high animal protein.

B004N44SOI|3.0|To begin, I must say that I loved the first Sixx A.M. album The Heroin Diaries which accompanied Nikki Sixx's book of the same name.  It didn't take more than a single listen of \\"Life is Beautiful\\" to become a fan of the band. At the time the concept of a soundtrack to a book was different and was a new concept to me, looking back I have to say it worked. Almost four years latter Sixx A.M.'s second album This Is Gonna Hurt is released, once again a book by the same name has been released shortly before. I really anticipated this second album as The Heroin Diaries was my favorite out of Nikki Sixx's side projects. The first Sixx A.M. album was fantastic; it was very honest, dark and twisted. I felt that it was reflective of Nikki's life and I could relate to that (and so did many other fans).<br /><br />The title track kick starts the album with a bang, this is an anthem and fist-pumping track, it has a \\"you and me against the world\\" type of feeling to it. Unfortunately, the title track is a heavy and rocking as the album gets, everything that follows it is slower or softer, not heavier. When I first heard the single, \\"Lies of the Beautiful People\\" I admit that I was slightly disappointed but the song grew on me and gets better with each listen.  \\"Are You With me\\" is I feel, one of the strongest songs of the album, I love the chorus the song just works. When James sings the chorus with the \\"come back from the dead\\" part I get chills, it's such a great vocal performance and so passionate. \\"Deadlihood\\" was another song I really liked. \\"Help Is On the Way\\" has a great message and it rocks, nice build-up to the chorus. \\"Skin\\" is a lyrical highlight for the band, the lyrics are so personal, honest and touching which was probably the point. It's also very simple with James singing, a piano and orchestra in the background. It closes the album nicely.<br /><br />This Is Gonna Hurt is overall a softer album and a bit less intense which is not necessarily a bad thing but I expected more rockers I suppose. This time around it feels somewhat less original; I don't have that special feeling, that connection with the lyrics from the first album. On its own This Is Gonna Hurt is a good album that has more of a modern feel to it. If you're a fan of the first I think this album is worth it as there are definitely some great songs worth listening to. I just wasn't blown away like I was with the first Sixx A.M. album, maybe if I never heard the debut I'd like this one much more. TIGH is a strong sophomore release from Sixx A.M., the band sounds tight. James Michaels proves himself once more as a vocalist and delivers a terrific performance. DJ Ashba is killer on guitar and Nikki is well...Nikki!<br /><br />This Is Gonna Hurt is very much a continuation of TIHD, it's similar in many ways and it has a depth and beautiful lyrics but I feel that this time around while it works, it doesn't work as well as it did with the first album. It's a good follow up to the band's first album and it has its moments, I wouldn't say that it's as good but the ride is still enjoyable this time around.  I don't know, perhaps it was the point I was at in my life when the album was released or maybe I identified more with The Heroin Diaries for some reason. I realize that I've made a lot of comparisons to the first album but that's just me, on its own body of work this is a very good album with once again some excellent lyrics, great guitar work and some solid vocals, it works.  I wouldn't say This Is Gonna Hurt amazed me and I can't say it's a disappointment either because there is a lot of enjoyable music here, I just feel some reviewers are rating it a little higher than it perhaps deserves. It sounds a lot like what the bands of today are coming up with, maybe a little too much. For me I'd say this is a 3 stars out of 5, enough to keep fans coming back.ers I suppose. Notably these are the \\"Live at LA Forum 1983\\" disc that came with the legacy edition of Def Leppard's \\"Pyromania\\" which actually sounds WORSE than the bootleg (how they managed that, I have no idea) and the Dio \\"Live At Donington\\" which I bought the Japanese SHM version and is only a slight improvement over the free trade versions. They did stick with a very live sound in the production. As is often the case with these Amazon reviews, the conversations get heated and quickly turn into personal jabs. It is a shame that too much knowledge of a certain subject can rob a person of the sheer enjoyment when that subject is presented on film. Oh Well, I am not a History Teacher, nor am I a Professor of any kind. I did not major in History in college, and what few classes I had to take, I memorized what I could for the exams and forgot the rest. Most of my college history courses were in Western History (Ancient Greek and Rome). I very much enjoyed this HBO mini-series. I just finished watching it today. I found it on a shelf in my mother's house and took an interest and I am so glad that I did. I found it to be very entertaining. I never read the book, on which this series is based, and so I cannot claim to be expert of its factuality or accuracy, however I was moved by this depiction of a man's self sacrifice for the creation and building of this great nation. I never considered the early, EARLY, developemental stages of the United States and thus this series really opened my eyes and forced me to regret -on some level- my own selfishness as a teenager in not focusing on my studies with the intention of giving my mind to the cause of the further development of my country. Although I did go to college, and manage to make Dean's a couple of times before graduating, this film really made me feel guilty about not doing more for America. Seeing how hard these men worked for this country and how rapidly we in these contemporary times are destroying it, I couldnt help but feel some sorrow for Adams. This was a man who had a respect for the law and for the Republic and for Independance -it made me sick, and at times nearly brought me to tears, when I think about the vast majority of this nation's inhabitants who have no respect for the Declaration of Independance and the Constitution of the United States.  It took such effort and such sacrifice for those two great documents to come into being. Well, getting off my soap box -I noticed some reviewers had a problem with the accuracy of the depiction of Adam's character. Again, having not read the book, I am in no position to argue the case, but I can say that I personally did not find this character to be unlikeable in anyway. I saw this character as a man who was trying to build a nation and free himself and his fellow man from the bonds of a strangling yet neglectful monarchy. I would think that one would have to be tough as stone and just as cold to make one's case and fight the good fight. If anything I found Jefferson's character to be too standoffish and not passionate nor active enough in the fight -this may not be historically accurate, but that was the impression I got from this series. In addition Franklin was presented as just plain decadent, indulgent and silly. Some people disliked Abigail's depiction and again I would have to disagree. I loved how she was strong willed, opinionated and yet refined and decent. She watched out for her man and stood by -as a spouse should -and watched her husband's faults. She was there to perfect her husband for his duty, whether or not she succeeded, she was a fantastic support and I loved her character. It was sweet and powerful when she passed away. She was such a force in Adam's life -according to the series. Finally, I noticed in the fight among the 1 star group that some disliked the scene where the man was tar and feathered. I found this scene to be very powerful and necessary for the drama -whether historically accurate, I do not know, but the series is for entertainment. The exposure and victimization of the gentleman really expressed the rage of the people and how they were fed up with the treatment by, and sheer arrogance of, the English Monarchy. Thewere driven insane by their rage.  I would recommend this series to anyone to watch as entertainment and as a reminder that many people fought, struggled and died for the birth of this great nation and we should love this country, it's laws and the ideas upon which it was founded.

0374531269|5.0|A long way gone by Ishmael Beah walks you through a tale of young boy and his friends trapped in between war, death and chaos. Beah successfully keeps you on the edge of your seat while leading you through a journey of misery and despair. This auto-biography puts to light the true meaning of war and the human condition. In \\"Memoirs of a Boy Soldier\\" Ishmael Beah depicts the utter devastation of war and the affect it has on the mind of the youth.    Beah proves the horror and atrocities committed to purely innocent people is what makes \\"war hell\\" and so gruesome that it disfigures everyone that encounters it. The author uses imagery to describe the extent of the war; he describes babies being covered from head to toe with bullets and boy used as a messenger from the rebels who had all of his fingers cut of instead of his thumbs. Beah uses many contrasting elements to further prove his point. He shows the difference between peace and war, and also life and death.   Ismael is mentally and physically trapped in the middle of the war. He loses contact with his friends and his family is burned to death. He is forced to fend for his own. He joins the army against the rebels and that is where he truly begins to lose the sense of who he is. He's unable to think while he's high on drugs and killing has become as easy as drinking water.  The only thing that can save Ismael is love, a love from a nurse and finally a mother that brings him to New York to tell his story. This is truly a captivating story that takes your breath away and makes you realize that there is always hope no matter how bleak the chances are.

The film blindsides you in the gut with a strong assault of emotion that makes you reminisce the best and most memorable Steven Spielberg films. B00004S35Z|5.0|Following a near fatal car accident it wasn't long before this album was written,recorded and released this album. B00004SZWD|5.0|Both of Stevie Wonder's previous two albums to this were one of the few in pop history that can truly be called musical masterpieces. From beginning to end they were highly consistant and original. But this was something different. Yes it was all those things too. But also it was an enormous labor of love,taking a than unbelievable 2 1/2 years to complete. With musicians such as Michael Sembello,Nathan Watts,Greg Phillinganes,Ronnie Foster and the late trumpet man Raymond Maldonado,along with a great many inspirational figures listed proudly in the album jacket this album spawned so much quality material that it sprawled a bit over the original double album lengh,including four songs that were originally included on a 45 inside the vinyl LP jacket. On this CD edition it's all in here in all it's glory. And it is glorious. The concept was the cycles of life,love and observation of the world and Wonder himself really put it all out here,no matter how long it took for the album to make it's appearance to the world. It was apparent that the lengh of time it took for this to come out was the result of how to the broad reaching subjects presented here accessible to most people. Also as previous musical ideas of Stevie's were being polished,arranged and combined into sleeker,more demanding sound ideas the end result was solid prood this was something worth waiting for.<br /><br />  Because of the amount of material I'll use two seperate paragraphs to describe each disc. The album starts out with the excellent,optimistic gospel styled pop/soul of \\"Loves In Need Of Love Today\\" and as nice a tune as it is it's STILL one of the weaker songs here if you can imagine. They are seperate songs but are part of a similar idea taken together. \\"Contusion\\" brings the band together for an uptempo jazz-funk/rock fusion instrumental jam and,if I may say so one of the most melodic of that form. Interestingly aside from the romantic midtempo grooves of \\"Knocks Me Off My Feet\\" the two hits on this part are two of the most compelling. \\"I Wish\\" marries pensive nostalgia with this great,playful shuffling funk while \\"Sir Duke\\" captures the flavor of..perhaps how Cab Calloway or Mr.Ellington himself might've sounded had they been recording at this time. The wistful funk groove of \\"Summer Soft\\" and the ballad/hard funk hyrbrid of \\"Ordinary Pain\\" are presented here in medley form,the former song talking of seasonal changes as living entities and the latter Stevie talking with his mascochistic female counterpart (as sung by Shirley Brewer).<br /><br />  On the second half we start out with \\"Isn't She Lovely\\",the bouncy celebration of birth and life that...yes has a well camouflaged goof in part of the harmonica solo. But Stevie's melodicism is so strong it totally disguises it from most people. \\"Joy Inside My Tears\\" and \\"If It's Magic\\",the latter featuring the late Detroit jazz harpist Dorothy Ashby both celebrate loves power to heal inner wounds married to two of his most dynamic arrangements in the form. \\"Black Man\\" is up to this point Stevie's most musically complex funk tune ever,a history lesson in song regarding the contributions of different people of different racial backrounds to American history with a likeminded musical array of different electronic synthesizer,early drum machine and vocorder effects that,to a degree predicts the electro funk movement of a few years from this point. He uses similar style in a more mambo soul style with \\"I Am Singing\\",where he sings the songs theme of musical as unifying force in Swahili,Spanish and English. It's in keeping with Stevie's concept of peoples differences being a cause for celebration. The midtempo jazz style soul of \\"As\\" is one of Stevie's most melodically complicated numbers,a personel favorite of mine and many and \\"Another Star\\" is a full band,uptempo latin funk celebration of love in all it's forms.<br /><br />  \\"Saturn\\",originally meant to refer to Stevie's home of Sagginaw is a dynamic midtempo ballad about the idea of an escape to ones private heaven whereas \\"Ebony Eyes\\",a jaunty and funky piano-soul tune celebrating a sort of school yard crush deals with a more worldly interest. On \\"All Day Sucker\\" Stevie again delivers a stuttering funk,with synths that sound a lot like turntable scratching in a proto hip-hop sense dealing with the preception of one as a sexual object. The instrumental \\"Easy Goin' Evening\\" is...well sort of a throwaway but again LOOK WHAT IT'S PART OF. It's sort of a cool down to this musicaly workout. From this point up to today Stevie Wonder's musical output slowed significantly-from an album a year to between five and even ten years sometimes between releases. And it's easy enough to see why. Here Stevie committed so many different lyrical,musical,meloldic and plain sound ideas onto his pallet that this could've actually served as his final album ever and he's still be a legend by the 80's. This album and many of these songs seperately have since become modern day standards. And even though MJ's [[ASIN:B00005QGAZ Thriller]] had higher sales figures this album achieved it's success,unlike that one not by premotion but rather by how it's related so heavily the listener-appealing to how our differences in love,life and consciousness made us what we are as people. 0070511136|4.0|The first thing you have to realize about \\"SPIN Selling\\" by Neil Rackham is that it's a book for selling to large accounts, written before anybody else was writing books about selling to large accounts.  Yes Virginia, selling to large accounts is different than selling to smaller ones.  To begin with, you cannot close the sale in one day, as a result opening the discussion by trying to closing technique is counterproductive.      Besides, in major sales, the salespeople usually are not in the room when most of the important decisions are made.  It's the salesperson's contact who needs to sell within her organization.  It's the job of the salesperson to arm his contact with enough information to make her an effective influencer.      Like any good book on sales, Rackham has methodology, in this case a four step questioning process.  In fact, the book gets its name from the four types of questions asked: Situational, Problem, Implication and Need Payoff.  Rackham says the key to sales is understanding where you are in the sales cycle - he identifies The Preliminaries, Investigating, Demonstrating Capability and Obtaining Commitment - and only to asking the kinds of questions that get you to the next level because sales in large accounts follow a specific sequence over a long timeline.     SPIN Selling process    Situational questions set the context for asking Problem questions that reveal implied needs, like the desire for a more accurate billing system.  Asking Implication questions about how many ways a more accurate would help the end-user's company lead to Need-Payoff questions, which help the buyer focus on solutions and benefits.      Speaking of benefits, Rackham notes that benefits come in two varieties: those that can help any customer and those that address your customer's express need. He differentiates the two types of \\"benefits\\" by calling the former an Advantage and the latter is a real Benefit.  Advantages help close small sales, while Benefits, whichaddress explicit buyers needs, help land big deals.     Yes, the book is dated.  Written 1988, it doesn't take into account that your customers already know a fair amount about your product from the Web.  At least somewhere in the questioning process, you have to determine how much your customer already knows about your product, if only to dispel mistaken assumptions. Additionally, while the book is very strong on tactics, it mistakenly assume you will only sell to someone lower in the company's organization.  It does not provide tactics on how to use the SPIN method to move up the company's organizational ladder, trying to get closer to the person who decides what projects get funded.    All of that being said, nearly 25 years after the book was published it is still a foundational book on selling to large account sales.  While the information available to customers has changed, the process for moving a customer through the sales cycle has not.      When the book was released, readers found that its theories ran counter to conventional wisdom because it deemphasized closing techniques in large sales.  At the time, most sales books focused on closing.  Actually, most sales training focused on closing because, as Rackham points out, closing used to be the one place in the sales cycle that had an understandable and quantifiable result.      But Rackham's studies show that trying to close too early alienates professional buyers, the very person who is almost certainly on the other end of the sales call at a large account.  Instead, the SPIN method relies on asking questions to tease out problems and help the client understand how the seller's solution can tip the value equation to the buy side of the ledger.      The foundation of  the book is rests on research conducted by Huthwaite corporation's 12-year $1 million research project into sales effectiveness, not just one excellent salesperson's career experience  This means the results of the book can be applied acrossa wide spectrum of businesses and industries, making a great starting point for learning about major account sales.    Now on the other hand, if you want to read anything about closing the quick sale, let me point you to either Tom Hopkins or  Zig Ziglar.

It is the third entry in the new mightily successful \\"Lincoln Lawyer\\" series by acclaimed American crime novelist Michael Connelly, who is also author of the best-selling detective Harry Bosch series of police procedurals. Both series are set in Los Angeles, California, and look at life on the \\"noir\\" -darker--side of that city.  Connelly is a former journalist, a crime beat writer for the Los Angeles Times, who certainly earned his spurs in murder while earning his daily bread.<br /><br />The Lincoln Lawyer novels center around criminal defense counsel Mickey Haller, whom devoted Connelly readers will know has actually been found to be a half-brother to Bosch: they were fathered by the same man, himself an outstanding LA defense counsel.  Mickey prefers to avoid the expense of maintaining a fixed office, as he finds it most efficient to work in the back seat of his Lincoln Town Car, while one of his former clients, unable to pay his bill, drives. If Mickey thought to see his criminal defense business grow during the recent economic downturn that hit LA, and the State of California, as hard as it hit anywhere else, he was mistaken: apparently, even people who need his help to keep themselves out of jail now have to watch their pennies.  In fact, the most significant part of Mickey's business now is not about helping to keep clients out of jail but about helping clients to keep a roof over their heads.  Because with bad times, foreclosures have boomed, and thousands of people who were granted unrealistic mortgages in the good times now face harrowing procedures. Lisa Trammel had been a client of Mickey's for eight months - his very first foreclosure case, in fact - and although so far he'd stopped the bank from taking her house, the sense of injustice, and the stress, are beginning to take their toll on his client.  The bank, in fact, had recently got a restraining order to prevent her protesting against their possibly fraudulent practices. Psychotic, stone cold killer Catherine Tramell in the 1990's film [[ASIN:078401020X Basic Instinct]]. We all lost fat around our temples, and have lots of sagging and wrinkles around our eyes. I am not sure there is a scientific explanation for this phenomenon, or if it is the kind of side effect usually associated with no-carb diets. This ought to be explored. To conclude, this diet, if you follow it strictly, will melt your extra pounds, but will make you look much older than you really are.
B001TKK3ES|5.0|I decided to write a bit about the vinyl edition of this masterpiece. Much of the info regarding the musical content has been described in the previous CD or MP3 reviews so I will keep this part light. Yeti is a fantastic listen... sprawling, meandering, intense... it is the kind of record that takes you on a audio journey, similar to Can's Future days in that regard. The two records break up the content very nicely... for this type of recording, the vinyl really shines and makes all the difference... Record one is contains the \\"compositions\\"... it is more \\"song\\" orientated and features more songs with lyrics.... great stuff. Record two is the \\"improvisations\\" sides.... awe-inspiring jams that are simply breathtaking.  Amon Duul II recorded and edited this with the vinyl format in mind and it is, in my opinion, the way to experience it.   Revisited did a nice job with the packaging... the cover printing is clear and correct looking. It is a great reissue that has new liner notes about the recording, etc... a nice touch. My only gripe about this reissue is the way in which the notes were handled. On the original LP, the images/collages were the visual counterpart to the music on the discs... they are great images, but they are now hidden behind the writings.... obscured. An insert containing the new liner notes would have been a better choice here... keep the artwork clean and visible while delivering the content elsewhere. Amon Duul II was the radiohead of their day, constructing the music and visual packaging as part of a complete piece of art. The treatment of the notes distracts a bit from this experience. I am glad they were included... I just wish it was done with more sensitivity.  That being said, I love this record. The original versions go for $80+ which is simply out of my price range. I pre-ordered this as soon as I heard about it and am very satisfied with it. It sounds amazing. The discs themselves are black 140 gram vinyl that were remastered by the band at an unspecified date. The music is clear and loud with no distortion or noise between tracks. Quality sound all around.   If you are thinking of picking this up... stop thinking and do it. For $24, this is a steal.  You will not be disappointed. Prepare for Yetification.

Other reviews recapped the novel, so I'll skip a summary and jump right into my comments. I do not think this book was perfect, I do not think the narration from the point of view of a child was flawless, but there was something about this book that really struck me. For the first five years of his life, Jack has never been outside of the 11x11 room, he has never seen the outside. He has never seen another human being besides his Ma, Old Nick and characters on tv. So there is a sweet innocence and naivete in Jack's perceptions of the world. I agree with other reviewers who could not believe that Ma was willing to put Jack into such danger in order to try to escape. Astounding? Yes. Unfathomable? No. Here is a woman who we come to learn has been held a prisoner in this shed for seven years. Jack's rescue mission was their only chance to escape, to survive. Yes, Jack started making grammatical mistakes when speaking that sounded more like a 3 year old than a 5 year old, but this made sense to me. It is completely normal for children who experience trauma to regress - and hasnt Jack experienced enough life-shattered events: life in Room for starters - sleeping in the wardrobe, hearing Ma repeatedly raped; then learning the life he knew wasnt exactly true life; the escape; the while process of acclimating to reality: seeing the world, meeting people etc. I would have found it stranger for Donaghue to ignore all of this trauma and have Jack go on to lead a normal life.     All in all, I thought this book was fantastic and a great psychological thriller. It would make an excellent movie!

Trey has mentioned many times in the past that he very much enjoys hardcore techno, industrial and IDM, and that he wanted to incorporate those influences into his work.  For Trey and Co. to sit back and write another Heretic style album would be very disappointing.  They fortunately did the complete opposite.  They wrote an album that is bold, unique and daring.  It took a lot of guts to do this and to be true to themselves, not to the extreme metal community.  Trey actually pokes fun at this with the track entitled 'Too Extreme!' As for the album itself, it might be their best produced ever.  The mixing of IDM and death metal never sounded better.  This is most prevelant on tracks like Too Extreme! and Destructo v. The Earth, which are the more daring tracks on the album (after multiple listens, I still find Radikult a little hard to swallow).  The rest are mostly killer death metal tracks that would sit nicely in a post Domination era David Vincent period, if it existed.  His voice has held up extremely well too.  I was very satisfied with Tim Yeung's drumming as well and he is a worthy sucsessor to Pete Sandoval's weighty shoes.  Overall, the album is extremely well written, fantastically produced, and represents the band as who they truly are in 2011.  Death metal purists might feel slighted but extreme metal fans with a hunger for something fresh and creative will rejoice.  Hailz Morbid Angel!!!

038572179X|4.0|We have all felt the burden of a guilty conscience; guilt burrows deep into our minds, threatening to never leave. This especially human emotion, along with many others, is captured in Ian McEwan's classic novel. Atonement is a tale of love, loss, and life itself set in a lush English country side then a war torn France and London in the WWII era. McEwan is a brilliant writer who clearly paints the novel's settings in his readers' minds, uses diverse narration, and doesn't shy away from delving very deep into the minds of the engrossing characters he has meticulously developed.            Robbie Turner is the intelligent yet low class Cambridge student who quickly discovers he has feelings towards Cecilia Tallis, the girl from a rich, well-to-do family which Robbie's mother is employed by. Perhaps the most memorable and scene-stealing character to live on the pages of this novel, however, is a thirteen year old play-write by the name of Briony Tallis. Her brilliantly wired mind continuously races from thought to thought, and she struggles to understand the world of the adults that surround her. McEwan's narration is refreshingly diverse, especially towards the begging of the novel when he switches from Robbie's infatuated state to Cecilia's stubborn character and finally strips away these facades to reveal a powerful romance and bring to life one of the greatest love stories to ever be told. A short but mesmerizing library scene in which Robbie and Cecilia first reveal their true feelings to one another, for example, goes a long way in establishing McEwan's ability as a writer. The tragedy sets in when Briony feels obligated to stop the relationship she sees to be unfit between her sister and Robbie. She quickly becomes convinced that Robbie is a \\"sex-maniac\\" and determinedly sets the course of his life into one of utter misery. Atonement is an infinitely better version of a Nicholas Sparks novel--its share of heartache is part of the story that is a classic.

B004K4AVAG|5.0|And that compliment is coming from someone who labeled the album's first single, the title track `Born This Way', a complete failure and a horrid rip-off misstep by the controversial artist. It's like Gaga is either completely brilliant or just plain awful.  Thankfully there are fourteen tracks on `Born This Way' and I only consider three of them to be terrible (or in that ballpark).<br /><br />You do the math.<br /><br />Consider the singles first.  First there was that awful `Born This Way', but I said all I have to say about that track on my review of the single.  Forget about that song.  Move onto the controversially brilliant `Judas', a song that attacks religious overbearance with fervor and an oddly singed eerie flare.  The bridge is flawless, and while the song has called comparisons to Gaga's own `Bad Romance', I see such originality and style oozing out of this track.  Still, `The Edge of Glory', which closes out the album, is still my favorite thing about the album.  I love everything about it.  The chorus is sharp and catchy; the verse structure is uplifting and fun and that saxiphone breakdown is just so richly original.  This is why Lady Gaga is relevant today!<br /><br />I know that `You and I' is not a `single' yet (or maybe it never will be), but I consider it as such thanks to Haley Reinhart's performance on American Idol. Still, it's a GREAT song and Gaga delivers a different side to herself that gives her yet another layer.  I mean, the honky-tonk nature of this song is so far removed from the industrial power of `Judas' or the 80's throwback of `Born This Way'.<br /><br />You cannot deny Gaga her versatility, even if you once wanted to pigeonhole her.<br /><br />`Americano' is laced with Latin influence, and where I felt `Alejandro' failed to impress (I know, everyone else seems to love that song, including my wife), `Americano' is practically FLAWLESS.  It is high spirited, full of lush energy and just bursting at the seams with intoxication.  `Schei&szlig;e' is got such slick edge thanks to the German interlude that rides itself throughout the duration of the track.  The techno base is well used and the rave worthy musical arrangement never disappoints.  This is Gaga paying homage to the great Madonna without crossing over the line of near plagiarism and thus losing her relevance.  `Schei&szlig;e' gets it.  It's a song that will grow and grow on you, much like `Government Hooker' will.  This is another song that laces itself with a gritty edge (and that sadomasochistic male vocal is undeniable).  The political statement made is perfectly executed without being preachy but instead becomes a part of Gaga's style.<br /><br />`Marry the Night' and `Electric Chapel' are both songs that I enjoy, but the fall slightly away from the `brilliant' term I was tossing at the majority of the album.<br /><br />They just feel slightly monotone.<br /><br />I'm on the fence about `Bloody Mary' because it is so stagnant for most of the track and then about two and a half minutes in the song becomes kind of amazing.  I'm sure after another five-hundred listens I'll think it's the best song on the album.  The lyrical content alone is kind of flawless.<br /><br />My friends totally disagree with me about `Highway Unicorn' and `Heavy Metal Lover'.  I love them, they consider them weak offerings.  I don't understand that assessment.  `Highway Unicorn' alone is like one of the greatest things ever.  I love the nostalgic feel to the track and yet it feels so crisp and modern. It feels like 80's pop/rock personified.  `Heavy Metal Lover' is also an animal all its own.  It doesn't feel `heavy' at all, but far more industrial with hushed tones and a unique nature that makes it stand out as separate from the rest of the album. B002MZKQ32|5.0|A Thousand Splendid Suns Review  By: Mariam     Treat a neighbor like family. To some, that seems like allowing a stranger into one's house, but sometimes taking a risk like that can leave a significant impact on one's life. Two neighbors who at first were complete strangers to one another, Mariam and Laila, create a relationship as they share their pains and life stories to one another and to the reader in the captivating novel of A Thousand Splendid Suns. Taken place in Afghanistan, author Khaled Hosseini uses a creative way to craft the individual stories behind the eyes of each character while combining them at the same time, allowing for an intriguing and emotional novel that leaves readers wanting to know what is yet to come.      Mariam is forced into a marriage with her husband Rasheed which soon becomes the same destiny as Mariam's neighbor, Laila, who is left parentless after an attack. Rasheed does nothing but add on to their already existing miseries of the constant outbreaks of war and Taliban with his constant degrading and abuse. As the book progresses, Hosseini references the different events Afghanistan faced throughout the years, making the novel more interesting by including history in these different women's stories. From the loss of a mother due to suicide to the attempt escape for hope in another country, the events in the novel will leave you unable to set the book down. With a combination of hate, grief, suspense, history, companionship, and love, Hosseini brings this fictional story to life with his amazing literature skills.     This book is highly unique. With its clever writing structure and amazing descriptions, it's difficult to put any negative comments towards it. I definitely recommend this book to all readers if they are truly interested in getting a taste out of a different culture as well as two heartfelt stories.

0307454541|4.0|Murder.  Corporate corruption.  Dark family secrets.  All three can be found within The Girl with the Dragon Tattoo, a modern masterpiece penned by Stieg Larsson.  Within the novel, Larsson is not only able to create a captivating crime drama that keeps the reader on the edge of their seat, but create a story that successfully combines three popular themes without overwhelming the reader.  Unlike other stories, these multiple subplots never tangle and cause the story to lag or grind to a halt; on the contrary, Larsson has structured them so they smoothly transition into one another to keep the story moving, and to keep the action going.<br /><br />  The characters within the story are also captivating as well, and human for all their quirks.  Both Blomkvist and Salander have their personal strengths and flaws, and Larsson makes no attempt to sugar coat their failings as a way to garter them sympathy.  Rather, the reader comes to sympathize with the characters after watching the story unfold, rather than having Larsson tell them to do so.  This pattern of showing, not necessarily telling, gives the reader freedom to guess and try to solve the many mysteries within the book themselves, drawing them into the story.  This higher level of reader involvement is what makes the novel so captivating, and so worthy of the popularity and notoriety it has received.

B000002MJ0|5.0|Sounding as if fragments of crystal were being levitated into the air and whirled around to resonate at a high frequency, \\"All I Need\\", the fifth track from \\"Isn't Anything\\" owes a debt of inspiration to one of My Bloody Valentine's musical touchstones, the Jesus and Mary Chain. Anyone who began listening to MBV after the release of \\"Loveless\\" and expected \\"Isn't Anything\\" to match its seamless, pristine values of production is going to be in for a bit of a shock. And while you can hear the underpinnings of the most heavily, gorgeously overlayered textures so predominant on \\"Loveless\\" within the guitar work on \\"Isn't Anything\\", the, gelled, embroidered quality of that sound is nascent and the riffs punchier or much more aggressive (just listen to \\"Sueisfine\\", \\"Feed Me With Your Kiss\\", or \\"You Never Should\\" if you need any confirmation). \\"Soft as Snow\\" , the opening track combines both of these elements, though not with the glistening production that characterized \\"Loveless\\"; instead, it's a little murkier, a little heavier, and to some who have complained about the production values, muddier.     \\"Several Girls Galore\\", the track that served as my introduction to the band, is perhaps a perfect example. Raw, heavy with distortion, squalling at times, and with a swirling directionality (though nowhere nearly as aggressively paced as the track that follows it, \\"You Never Should\\") underneath the sculpted quality of the cut lies a tunefulness that the band would mine to a much greater degree on \\"Loveless\\".     Yet as much as MBV drew inspiration from the JAMC's heavily distorted, tuneful walls of feedback, a deep appreciation for the ethereal quality of the ouvre of Cocteau Twins was as equally responsible for the shimmering aspect brought to bear in \\"Loveless\\" , but again hinted at strongly on \\"Isn't Anything\\", especially on \\"No More Sorry\\" and \\"Lose My Breath\\".     And listening with an attentive ear (as well as a pair of quality headphones) can result in revealing newly successive wrinkles on the first few runs through the CD. \\"Isn't Anything\\" isn't a release that's designed to promote a casual listening experience. It's not party music. It could be said to more of a deconstruction of the Jesus and Mary Chain and the Cocteau Twins, or a blueprint for the shoegazing movement that would come into prominence after the release of this CD, thanks largely in part to the release OF \\"Isn't Anything\\". This is my third copy in the past 21 years, and it still is in rotation within my car CD changer. I would definitely recommend getting a copy, especially if you already own \\"Loveless\\" and are on the fence about this one.

0553386794|4.0|I was always reluctant to read this book, as I have been to read most medieval world type fantasy after Tolkien ([[ASIN:0345340426 J.R.R. Tolkien Boxed Set (The Hobbit and The Lord of the Rings)]]) and E. R. Eddison ([[ASIN:073510171X The Worm Ouroboros]]). So much of it was derivative (dwarfs and goblins, elves and wizards, great quests and magical talismans and the opposition of world threatening dark forces). A few early samplings of the post-Tolkien crowd back in the eighties led me to conclude there was no matching the masters. But the recent HBO series dramatizing this book (the first in an apparently still unfinished series by George R. R. Martin) prompted me to think again. I've found the made for television series richly drawn, its character-driven tale compelling and its background faux medieval world remarkably distinctive and detailed.<br /><br />So I ordered the books from amazon. This, the first of the series of four books so far, has lived up to my expectations.<br /><br />A complex tale of political intrigue concerning the rule of the Seven Kingdoms, A Game of Thrones introduces us to a world that has been superimposed on the bones of England's late medieval period. The Seven Kingdoms are situated on an island of undetermined size off the coast of a much larger land mass, separated by the \\"Narrow Sea\\". The country is rather like a mix of dark age England with Norse-like people occupying the north and a high medieval culture in the south. The capital, Kings Landing (situated approximately where London is in the real England), has an almost Mediterrannean air to it.<br /><br />The land of the Seven Kingdoms has two main seasons, winter and summer, and each lasts many years and, we are told, \\"winter is coming\\". The story opens fifteen years after the overthrow of the \\"Mad King\\" Aerys Targaryen, a tyrant against whom the lords of the realm (many whose ancestors were once kings in their own right) had risen up. The Targaryens were bloodily expunged in the rebellion and the throne taken by one Robert Baratheon of the ancient House Baratheon. King Robert, now fifteen years older, a good deal fatter and utterly disgusted with the responsibilities of kingship (for which he is little suited) has come north to recruit his old friend Lord Eddard Stark to help him rule at King's Landing.<br /><br />Lord Stark (Ned to his wife and the King) is reluctant to accept the charge to become King's Hand but honor finally compels him and he begins his journey south with the king and his dysfunctional family, a journey that will precipitate a series of catastrophic events for the kingdom itself. Based loosely on the strife that ripped England apart during the War of the Roses, the two main contending houses of the future conflict are Ned's own House Stark and that of the king's wife, Queen Cersei: House Lannister. (The main houses in the War of the Roses were the Yorks and the Lancasters.)<br /><br />Like England, this land is of ancient lineage. Originally occupied by the Children of the Forest (a primitive people of small stature) it was later taken and ruled by the First Men who arrived from oversea to set up the early kingdoms. The First Men were followed by the Andals who built the culture and civilization which undergirds the land at the time the story commences. Like England, which was conquered and ruled by the invading Duke William Bastard of Normandy, but whose line eventually collapsed, so the land of the Seven Kingdoms was subsequently conquered by one Aegon the Conqueror who managed to suppress the native kings of Andal and the First Men with his host of flying fire breathing dragons.<br /><br />And so the fantasy finds its way into the tale. This world looks superficially like our own in high medieval times but is anything but. Naturally, though, religion plays a role. The New Faith, brought by the Andals, worships the Seven Faces of God inside septs (churches) and is guided by a religious order modeled after the medieval Church, while the Old Faith, still strong in the north, worships many distinct gods of near forgotten identity and nature, but it is a faith inherited by the First Men from the Children of the Forest and is still strong in the north. Worship in the old way requires a godswood and so many of the strongholds have their own little forest preserve within their walls, along with a proper sept, and far to the north, where a great wall stretches to secure the realm from the wild frozen lands north of it, the old gods still hold sway. What has nearly been forgotten, however, except for ancient tales now generally disbelieved, is the existence of beings called the White Walkers who are said to slumber for thousands of years under the heavy ice before each reappearance. These strange beings are said to walk the earth only in the coldest of times, drinking the blood of men. And now, as the Stark House motto has it, \\"Winter is Coming\\" again, and all indications are that it will be a very long one.<br /><br />The great wall, constructed in an age barely recalled at the time of the story, holds back the wild barbarians who still live north of the wall and, if legend is to be credited, the dread White Walkers who haven't been seen in so long that they are now generally discounted by all and sundry. Even the men of the Night's Watch, outcasts and lawbreakers all who have \\"taken the black\\" to expunge their crimes and thereby dedicated themselves to an isolated existence guarding the Wall, seem to have forgotten. But there are bits of evidence which some refuse to credit that warn that the White Walkers may be waking.<br /><br />As Ned Stark heads south with King Robert, the intrigue rapidly unfolds while Stark family members are quickly scattered to various parts of the realm, never to be reunited in happy unity again. Ned's bastard son, Jon Snow, must \\"take the black\\" and dedicate his life to defense of the Wall, for want ot a place in his father's stronghold, Winterfell, now that Lord Stark has gone, while the two Stark daughters have gone south, with a large part of the household, to serve the king. Elder daughter, Sansa, a red haired beauty not yet of marriageable age has been pledged to King Robert's vain and handsome young son, Prince Joffrey, while younger daughter Arya, a tough little tomboy bridles at the courtly airs and inevitable restrictions the king's household imposes on them. Ned's three sons and his wife remain behind at Winterfell. But events will soon split them, even further, as Ned discovers the poisonous personalities and ambitions around the king and tries to set things right by discovering what really happened to the previous King's Hand, Jon Arryn, who had been foster father to both Ned and Robert in their youth.<br /><br />The supporting characters are fully as complex as the primary ones. King Robert's wife, the icy beauty Queen Cersei Lannister, has a twin brother, Jaime of the golden hair, a hero of the realm, albeit one with a dark stain upon his name for he had been a member of the old King's Guard fifteen years earlier and betrayed the Mad King by assassination in order to join with Robert Baratheon, Eddard Stark and Jon Arryn in their uprising. Jaime's brother is a twisted dwarf, Tyrion Lannister, with a clever mind, wry sense of humor and a cynical soul reflecting the misfortune of his birth and a father, Lord Tywin Lannister, who cannot abide his imperfection.<br /><br />Lady Catelyn, Ned's wife, had once been betrothed to Ned's older brother, a man who, along with Ned's father and sister, had been slain by the Mad King. As Ned rides south with the increasingly troubled King Robert and his unruly household, across the sea to the east another plots his return. The Mad King left two children on his death who had been spirited away by their protector in the disorder of the rebellion. Raised in poverty but with hopes of reclaiming the Iron Throne, constructed by Aegon the Conqueror in the long ago time of the melted swords of his vanquished foes, Prince Viserys schemes to sell his fourteen year old younger sister, Danerys Targaryen, to Khal Drogo of the Dothraki horde, a nomad king who commands some forty thousand barbarian horsemen. The khal is to get the innocent, virgin princess in exchange for a promise to lend Viserys his horde for reconquest of the Seven Kingdoms.<br /><br />Nor does the story exhaust itself on these events and personalities alone. Back in King's Landing there is the Small Council which serves the king, consisting of certain high men of the realm including the king's youngest brother, a scheming eunuch, a shrewd lord and businessman who had once loved Catelyn Stark, an aging Maester (member of an ancient religious order which perpetuates the realm's learning) and the head of the king's own guard. All of these plus a good many other interesting characters (including the Braavosi swordmaster Syrio Forel, recruited by Lord Stark to teach his younger daughter to defend herself) drive the fast moving plot forward.<br /><br />And there are certainly villains, like Sandor Clegane, the Hound of Prince Joffrey, and his brother, Ser Gregor Clegane, the Man Mountain, though not all are as obviously reprehensible as these two (although even Sandor, we learn, has his almost sympathetic side). Perhaps the least satisfying character of all is the shallow and remarkably simple Sansa, Lord Stark's older daughter, who continues to dream her romantic fantasies about marriage to the Prince, despite the growing evidence that he is not what she imagines. It's hard to believe a Stark child raised in the surroundings of Winterfell with the shrewd counsel of their resident maester could be so naive and utterly blind to events around her. And there is a strange device of carrier ravens which miraculously manage to move about from keep to keep, camp to camp, as a sort of messenger service that keeps the story moving without benefit of telephones or telegrams.<br /><br />But overall the story works on a multiplicity of levels. While it lacks the powerful emotional resonance of a book like Hope Muntz' [[ASIN:B001DA6IKC The Golden Warrior, the Story of Harold and William, a Novel of the Norman Conquest]] (which tells of the real intrigue that led to the accession to the English throne of William the Conqueror in 1066), this is a robustly and richly imagined work with just enough of the fantastic to justify its inclusion in the epic fantasy genre. It's not Tolkien or Eddison, to be sure, but it's no pale imitation of them either.<br /><br />Stuart W. We have heard that from Simon before, in \\"Have a Good Time,\\" from his mid-1970s album \\"Still Crazy After All These Years.\\" This time, however, Simon is not a frenetic youth but a mature man reflecting on nearly seventy years of life. Music aficionados will enjoy the unexpected appearance of a kora and mouthed Indian percussion.     Simon has extended the theme in \\"Slip-Slidin' Away\\" to album length. It is Simon's most fully integrated work, so far, and that's sayin' a mouthful.

B000LPQ6DE|2.0|Before the latest Masterpiece Theater version of Jane Eyre was even broadcast, it was already obvious that the casting director for this particular adaptation had not actually read the book. The actress who plays Jane looks more like a sturdy, red-cheeked Kansas farm girl than the young woman described by Charlotte Bronte. Jane spent her early years in a damp, disease-ridden Victorian orphanage in England. It was, in short, a little death house -- as were many Victorian orphanages. The miracle is that Jane not only survived childhood in such an institution, but was able to gain a modest education.   Charlotte Bronte takes particular care in describing the contrast between Jane's small, pale appearance and the strength of her character.  Jane would not have got enough to eat as a child, and this would have stunted her growth and resulted in the frailness that Bronte describes so clearly (Victorians in general were smaller than their predecessors). This, of course, is central to the dynamic between the physically brutish Rochester, whose past life of dissolution and weakness has brought disaster upon him.  Jane, a little wraith who frightens Rochester's horse because she seems as if from another world, has a will as strong as tempered steel. By casting an actress who galumphs around like a carthorse (albeit a pretty one), the physical contrast so central to the book -- that between Jane and Rochester -- is completely lost. The story of Jane Eyre has always appealed to women because the heroine was not conventionally beautiful, yet she triumphs over tragic circumstances through the sheer force of her spirit. Suggest reading the book. Thankfully, the latest movie version, I believe, stars an Eastern European actress who at least appears to be satisfyingly thin.
